    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="e3f1fc28223845caa052e1fc61faf24c"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="e3f1fc28223845caa052e1fc61faf24c">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '12783133-e1bd-4d51-951f-70b4082c7bf1'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d8485448" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>A nitrogen-doped carbon layer coating on a 3D graphene framework (NCL@GF) was produced by the in-situ polymerization of a uniform polyimide layer on the graphene framework surface and a carbonization process. The NCL@GF exhibited a 3D macroporous structure with uniform N-doped porous carbon layers and a high 5.4 at. % nitrogen content, which not only effectively enhanced the active sites but also facilitated fast ion and electron transport in the 3D pathways. Consequently, the NCL@GF as the anode of a sodium-ion battery (SIB) exhibited a discharge capacity of 357 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles, a remarkable rate capability with a capacity of 122 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 8 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and a super long cycle life with a capacity retention of 70 % capacity after 2500 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Such performance is superior to that of previously reported carbon or graphene-based composites.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="asia201801484-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="asia201801484-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Over the past several decades, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been known as the major power supplies for electric vehicles and electronic devices because of their high energy density and stable cyclic performance, etc.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0001_R_d8485440e439" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Mater.">1</a>, <a href="#asia201801484-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0002_R_d8485440e439" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></span> Despite these advantages, the limited lithium resource on earth restricts the applications of LIBs severely. In view of this, researchers have made great efforts to explore alternative energy storage systems.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0003_R_d8485440e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#asia201801484-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0004_R_d8485440e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater.">4</a></span> Nowadays, they have paid more attention to sodium ion batteries (SIBs), because of the more abundance in nature and the much lower cost of sodium than lithium.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0005_R_d8485440e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">5</a>, <a href="#asia201801484-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0006_R_d8485440e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Mater. Chem. A">6</a></span> However, only a few anode materials are suitable for SIB, because of larger radius of Na than Li, which would suffer from more serious volume expansion during the charge/discharge process and lead to more sluggish sodium kinetics, more difficult intercalation into the lattice and lower diffusion in the electrode materials.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0007_R_d8485440e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater.">7</a></span> Such a very limited number of various SIB anode materials, carbon nanomaterials, which are accompanied with earth-abundant, eco-friendly, potential low cost and electrically conductive, are considered as one of the most competitive candidates.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0003_R_d8485440e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#asia201801484-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0006_R_d8485440e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Mater. Chem. A">6</a>, <a href="#asia201801484-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0008_R_d8485440e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">8</a>-<a href="#asia201801484-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0013_R_d8485440e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Small">13</a></span> However, these materials show a low discharge capacity in SIBs (approximately 350 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) because of the poor insertion kinetics of Na ions into these nanomaterials.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0013_R_d8485440e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Small">13</a></span> The construction of three-dimensional (3D) and two-dimensional (2D) structure of carbon-based anode materials for SIB, which can effectively enhance the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation of into the carbon interlayer by shortening the ion diffusion distance and the rapid charge transfer on the surface.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0006_R_d8485440e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Mater. Chem. A">6</a>, <a href="#asia201801484-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0014_R_d8485440e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a></span> Furthermore, the carbon materials with heteroatom doped and nanopore structure have been proved to significantly increase surface wettability and improve the electrical conductivity of the electrodes to promote electrode-electrolyte interactions, therefore, can help generate high sodium storage capacity through surface adsorption and faradic reaction. Multifarious ways have been proposed to get heteroatom-doped porous carbon materials.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0015_R_d8485440e458" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Electrochim. Acta">15</a>-<a href="#asia201801484-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0017_R_d8485440e458" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - RSC Adv.">17</a></span> Organic polymers with the robust framework are usually used as the precursor for the creation of heteroatom-enriched porous carbon. Especially, Polyimide (PI) has been successfully converted to N-doped porous carbon, which showed excellent electrochemical performance as anode for LIB.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0002_R_d8485440e460" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></span> However, the N-doped carbon hybrids derived from PI as the anode for SIB has never been reported.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we facilitated thick N-doped carbon layer (≈6 nm) growth in 3D graphene frameworks (NCL@GF) with a high 5.4 at. % nitrogen content via in situ polymerization of PI layers on graphene template and self-assembly of graphene into 3D network structure simultaneously followed by solvothermal and carbonization processes. PI layer was in situ polymerized on the graphene template with the monomers of pyromellitic dianhydride (PMDA) and 2,6-diaminoanthraquinone (DAAQ) via solvothermal. After carbonization, the N-doped porous carbon layers, derived from PI, covered on the graphene framework surface which can provide more spaces for Na storage through surface adsorption and faradic reaction.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0006_R_d8485440e465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Mater. Chem. A">6</a></span> The 3D graphene framework in the composites increased the contact surface with the electrolyte, and improved conductivity, thus facilitated the rapid carrier transport on the interface and shorten ion diffusion from the electrolyte to the electrode. Benefiting from the synergistic effect, as an electrode material for SIBs, the NCL@GF exhibited a high reversibly capacity of 357 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles, a good rate capacity of 122 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 8 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and an excellent stability with a capacity retention of 70 % capacity after 2500 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. To the best of our knowledge, the performance of NCL@GF achieved in our work is almost superior to that for previously reported carbon-based anode materials for SIB in Table S1 (Supporting Information).</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="asia201801484-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="asia201801484-sec-0002-title"> Result and discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The fabrication of the NCL@GF is illustrated in Figure <a href="#asia201801484-fig-0001">1</a> a. First, PMDA and DAAQ were dissolved in a suspension of graphene oxide (GO) by sonicating for 30 min with high concentration. The monomers of PMDA and DAAQ with aromatic structure, both can be absorbed onto the GO surface by π–π interactions. Then, the thick PI layer finally can be tightly in situ polymerization on the graphene template by strong π–π interactions via solvothermal treatment, which led to the simultaneous self-assemble of 3D frameworks (PI@GF). Finally, the NCL@GF with N-doped carbon layer was obtained after the carbonization of PI@GF with N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere. In the control experiments, PMDA and ethylene diamine (EDA) without aromatic structure were used as the monomers (Figure <a href="#asia201801484-fig-0001">1</a> b). Only the PMDA monomer can absorb on the graphene surface. The π–π interactions between the monomers and graphene were not strong, resulting in PI with flowers-like particles form on graphene surface by solvothermal treatment. And finally, the N-doped carbon flowers (NCF) growth on 3D graphene framework (NCF-GF) was also obtained after carbonization treatment.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0002_R_d8485440e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#asia201801484-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0018_R_d8485440e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Mater. Chem. A">18</a></span> The GF without the N-doped carbon layer was also fabricated by hydrothermal of GO.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="asia201801484-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c84e5409-3fab-49e6-ad22-e983093e4432/asia201801484-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c84e5409-3fab-49e6-ad22-e983093e4432/asia201801484-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d8185bbf-7579-41a6-83b6-ef954fcdb3f9/asia201801484-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c84e5409-3fab-49e6-ad22-e983093e4432/asia201801484-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=asia201801484-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fasia.201801484" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) Scheme of NCL@GF and the polymerization of the PI with the monomers of PMDA and DAAQ, b) Scheme of NCF-GF and the polymerization of the PI with the monomers of PMDA and EDA.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The morphology of the GF, NCL@GF and NCF-GF were investigated by scanning electron microscope (SEM), transmission electron microscope (TEM) and atom force microscope (AFM). In the SEM images in Figure <a href="#asia201801484-fig-0002">2</a>, the highly interconnected 3D frameworks with pore sizes of micrometers are visible in all three samples. These findings indicated that the in situ polymerization of PI on the graphene surface and the carbonization process did not influence the 3D self-assembly or destroy the 3D architecture. The SEM and TEM images of the GF showed that the graphene sheet looked ultra-thin, where only a large number of wrinkles were found (Figure <a href="#asia201801484-fig-0002">2</a> a–b). In addition, the AFM image showed that the thickness of graphene was only approximately 0.7 nm (Figure <a href="#asia201801484-fig-0002">2</a> c), which well-matched our previous studies.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0019_R_d8485440e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Nano">19</a></span> For the NCL@GF samples, both the SEM and TEM results showed that the uniform and compact carbon layer coated the graphene surface (Figure <a href="#asia201801484-fig-0002">2</a> d–f), indicating that the polymerization of the PI layer successfully covered on both sides of the graphene surface through π–π interactions between the aromatic structure monomers (PMDA and DAAQ) and graphene. The AFM image further revealed that the wall thickness of the NCL@GF was approximately 6 nm, which was thicker than the GF (≈0.7 nm) (Figure <a href="#asia201801484-fig-0002">2</a> g). Thus, these results unambiguously validated that an additional N-doped carbon layer was successfully introduced with a thickness of about 2.6 nm on both wall sides of the NCL@GF. The thickness of the N-doped carbon layer can be easily controlled by changing the concentration of the monomers in the precursor solution. For example, changing the concentration of monomers from 0.4 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> to 0.8 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> and 0.03 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> can adjust the layer thickness from 6 nm to 10 nm (NCL@GF-thick) and 4 nm (NCL@GF-thin), respectively (Figure S1). For the NCF-GF, the SEM images showed that the flowers-like particles of N-doped carbon were hung onto the graphene framework surface (Figure <a href="#asia201801484-fig-0002">2</a> h–i). The different formation mechanisms of NCF-GF and NCL@GF were illustrated as follows: For the NCF-GF composites, only the monomers of PMDA adsorbed onto the graphene surface, while the EDA without the aromatic structure dispersed into the solution. It led to the polymerization of flower-like PI on the graphene surface. And the flower-like PI was derived as the carbon particles after pyrolysis. For the NCL@GF composites, both PMDA and DAAQ with high concentration and aromatic structures, were absorbed on the graphene surface by π–π interactions and generated a large amount of polymeric active sites on the graphene surface.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0019_R_d8485440e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Nano">19</a></span> Thick PI layers were able to grow on both sides of the graphene framework and then were carbonized as the N-doped carbon layer on the graphene for the composites, which was beneficial to improving the conductivity.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="asia201801484-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a11c8b8e-56fd-4e6d-b2aa-08cf87842f6f/asia201801484-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a11c8b8e-56fd-4e6d-b2aa-08cf87842f6f/asia201801484-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/77102c00-5d52-4676-9902-34cc92175c37/asia201801484-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a11c8b8e-56fd-4e6d-b2aa-08cf87842f6f/asia201801484-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=asia201801484-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fasia.201801484" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a–c) SEM, TEM and AFM images of GF; (d,e) SEM, (f) TEM and (g) AFM image of NCL@GF. (h,i) SEM images of NCF-GF.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The crystal structures of GF, NCL@GF and NCF-GF were characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD, Figure <a href="#asia201801484-fig-0003">3</a> a). There were two diffraction peaks centered at ca. 24° and 43° in GF, and belong to the (002) peak and the (100) lattice planes of graphite crystal, corresponding to the stack of graphene layers and ordered structure of carbon six-membered rings, respectively.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0006_R_d8485440e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Mater. Chem. A">6</a>, <a href="#asia201801484-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0021_R_d8485440e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nano Lett.">21</a></span> In contrast, only the broad peak at ca. 24° was observed in the NCL@GF and NCF-GF, implying an amorphous carbon phase.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0006_R_d8485440e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Mater. Chem. A">6</a>, <a href="#asia201801484-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0022_R_d8485440e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Small">22</a></span> These results indicated that the PI had been successfully converted to amorphous after carbon annealing process<sup>23</sup>. This finding was further elucidated by the Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectra of the composites before and after carbonization treatment (Figure <a href="#asia201801484-fig-0003">3</a> b). In PI and PI@GF samples, the peaks at 1650 and 1334 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponded to the C=O symmetric stretching vibration and C−N stretching vibration, respectively.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0017_R_d8485440e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - RSC Adv.">17</a></span> For the NCL@GF and NCF-GF samples, the disappearance of the peak at 1650 cm<sup>−1</sup>, further indicated that PI had been carbonized.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0023_R_d8485440e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Sci. Rep.">23</a></span> It was further demonstrated by Raman spectra of the NC@GF-<i>T</i> with visible D and G bands at about 1330 and 1600 cm<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure S2 a). The intensity ratio of the D to G band for NCL@GF and NCF-GF were around 1 indicating the N-doped may introduce the significant defects on carbon framework with the graphitic carbon environment.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0020_R_d8485440e589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a></span> The specific surface area of the NCL@GF was recorded up to 277 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> by Brunauer-Emmett-Teller (BET) method, which markedly contrasted that of GF (≈179 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) and NCF-GF (57 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) (Figure S2 b). This result indicated that the surface area of the hybrid materials effectively improves after coating a carbon layer on the 3D GF. There may be two reasons for the higher specific surface area of NCL@GF than GF: 1) The in situ polymerization of PI on the graphene surface, can effectively prevent π–π stacking of graphene. 2) The more defects were introduced to the graphene surface after the PI polymer layers converted to the carbon with pyrolysis. The hierarchical porous channels of NCL@GF can provide not only numerous nanopore for sodium ion intercalation but also facilitate pathways for electrolyte, thus giving larger specific capacities and improving the rate performance. The <i>x</i>-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was further presented to investigate the chemical nature of the NCL@GF and NCF-GF (Figure <a href="#asia201801484-fig-0003">3</a> c and d). The N contents of the NCL@GF and NCF-GF were calculated to be 5.79 and 8.80 at. %, respectively. The N1s spectra of NCL@GF and NCF-GF can be fitted to three peaks at ≈398.9, ≈400.2, and ≈401.0 eV, corresponding to pyridinic nitrogen, pyrrolic nitrogen, and graphitic nitrogen, respectively.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0023_R_d8485440e608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Sci. Rep.">23</a></span> Applied to the anode of SIBs, both the large surface area and the existence of considerable number of <i>N</i>-containing species of NCL@GF create more active sites for sodium storage by surface absorption and faradic reaction.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0024_R_d8485440e612" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Energy Environ. Sci.">24</a>, <a href="#asia201801484-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0025_R_d8485440e612" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="asia201801484-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/121b5261-4ba4-428f-9b2e-3f316d6d50bd/asia201801484-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/121b5261-4ba4-428f-9b2e-3f316d6d50bd/asia201801484-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d36d6d8f-12c1-4c88-b360-963231e2bd1e/asia201801484-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/121b5261-4ba4-428f-9b2e-3f316d6d50bd/asia201801484-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=asia201801484-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fasia.201801484" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) XRD patterns of GF, NCL@GF and NCF-GF, (b) FTIR spectrum of the GF, PI, PI@GF, NCL@GF and NCF-GF, c) XPS survey spectrum of NCL@GF and NCF-GF, (b) the high-resolution XPS N1s spectrum of the NCL@GF and NCF-GF.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Benefitting from the designed structure and high N-doping level, the as-prepared NCL@GF was suitable for use as an anode for SIBs. The initial five CV curves of NCL@GF at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> were revealed in Figure <a href="#asia201801484-fig-0004">4</a> a with a potential window of 0.01 to 3 V (vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) with the electrolyte. In the first cycles, the peaks during 1.1–0.4 V was corresponded to the formation of a solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0006_R_d8485440e643" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Mater. Chem. A">6</a></span> The large peak at 0.33 V in the reduction state and the peaks at 0.90 and 1.70 V in the oxidation state were observed, which could be attributed to the insertion and de-insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions into/from the carbon.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0025_R_d8485440e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a></span> From the second cycle forward, notably the CV curves were almost overlapped, indicating the superior reversibility of the NCL@GF.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0023_R_d8485440e650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Sci. Rep.">23</a></span> The charge-discharge profiles of the 1st, 2nd, 100th, 150th and 200th cycles of the NCL@GF electrode at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> were shown in Figure <a href="#asia201801484-fig-0004">4</a> b, which were similar to those previously reported for N-doped carbon materials.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0026_R_d8485440e656" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Sci.">26</a></span> The presence of a plateau at 1.1–0.4 V in the first discharge cycle was owned to the SEI layer formed on the electrode surface, which was consistent with CV results.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0023_R_d8485440e658" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Sci. Rep.">23</a></span> The first discharge and charge capacities of NCL@GF were 663.8 and 412 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The initial Coulombic Efficiency (CE) was calculated to be 62 %. The low CE of NCL@GF in the first cycle could be ascribed to the formation of the SEI and irreversible reactions on the surface of the materials. The discharge curves of the 100th, 150th and 200th cycles were almost coincident, which indicated that the electrochemical performance was very stable. The highly reversible capacity was attributed to the high N-doping, which provided abundant active sites for sodium. The large irreversible capacity was due to a large number of N-doping sites.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0025_R_d8485440e663" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a>, <a href="#asia201801484-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0027_R_d8485440e663" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Electrochim. Acta">27</a></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="asia201801484-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/18d3c789-0eab-453b-bd33-133825b975e3/asia201801484-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/18d3c789-0eab-453b-bd33-133825b975e3/asia201801484-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/903af29b-082d-4d8a-b785-fb4d2ea52a17/asia201801484-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/18d3c789-0eab-453b-bd33-133825b975e3/asia201801484-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=asia201801484-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fasia.201801484" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Electrochemical sodium storage properties of NCL@GF (a) CV curves of the NCL@GF electrode for initial 5 cycles at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> between 0.01 and 3 V. (b) The charge-discharge profiles of NCL@GF in the 1<sup>st</sup>, 2<sup>nd</sup>,100<sup>th</sup>, 150<sup>th</sup> and 200<sup>th</sup> cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. (c) Galvanostatic charge/discharge performance of NCL@GF, NCF-GF and GF electrodes at the current densities of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. (d) Comparison of the rate capacity of NCL@GF, NCF-GF and GF electrodes. (e) Rate performance of the NCL@GF electrode at current densities from 1 to 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The cycling performance of the NCL@GF was shown in Figure <a href="#asia201801484-fig-0004">4</a> c. The discharge capacity in the 1<sup>st</sup> cycle was 663 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and then slightly increased during the 10<sup>th</sup>–50<sup>th</sup> cycles. Even after 100<sup>th</sup> and 150<sup>th</sup> cycle, the discharge capacity stabilized at 366, and 367 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The composite still maintained a capacity of ∼357 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> with a CE of &gt;99 % after 200 cycles. The graphene framework matrix in the NCL@GF delayed electrolyte wetting into the active layers and buffered the volume change during the cycles. Thus, the capacity showed a slight increase up to 50 cycles and then a high stability. As control experiments, the electrochemical performance of NCF-GF and GF was also investigated with the same conditions (Figure <a href="#asia201801484-fig-0004">4</a> c, S3 and S4). Inferior electrochemical performance was obtained with the NCF-GF and GF composites, which delivered capacities of 241 and 45 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles. Further, we also prepared the N-doped carbon hybrids (NCF-GF-N<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub>) via the monomers of PMDA and hydrazine, and the non-N-doped carbon hybrid (CL@GF) via glucose as the carbon precursor. N-doped graphene framework (<i>N</i>-GF) without carbon layer was also directly prepared with GO and polyethylenimine (PEI) via hydrothermal treatment. These hybrids also showed the poor electrochemical performance. The capacities of NCF-GF-N<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub> and CL@GF samples were only 195 and 80 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles (Figure S3 and S4). Without the carbon layers coated on the graphene surface, the capacity of <i>N</i>-GF was only 88 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 80 cycles (Figure S5). The high performance of the NCL@GF was attributed to the synergistic effect of N-doped, carbon layers and 3D graphene frameworks, which were beneficial for enhancing the kinetics of the electrochemical effects.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0006_R_d8485440e755" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Mater. Chem. A">6</a></span></p>
         
         <p>More significantly, the NCL@GF electrode also showed an excellent rate performance (Figure <a href="#asia201801484-fig-0004">4</a> d). The NCL@GF electrode delivered the capacities of 247, 219, 190, 158, 122 and 90 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5, 1, 2, 4, 8 and 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The capacity could still recover to 257 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> when the current density was returned to 0.5 Ag<sup>−1</sup>. However, the NCF-GF showed low capacity of 65 at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Without N-doping in the structure, the GF delivered only 4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The reason was that the N-doping in the structures improved the electrochemical performance. Compared with the NC flower-like particles hanging on the 3D graphene frameworks in NCF-GF, the thick NC layers were covered on the surface of the graphene frameworks in the NCL@GF, which can significantly improve the fast charging performance and effectively protect the structure (Figure S6).<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0006_R_d8485440e776" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Mater. Chem. A">6</a></span> We also had prepared an <b>ultrathin</b> N-doped carbon nanosheets supported on 3D graphene framework before. It's applied to an anode electrode for LIB.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0022_R_d8485440e780" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Small">22</a></span> But the ultrathin nanosheets may not good as the SIB anode electrode, because of Na<sup>+</sup> with larger radius than Li<sup>+</sup>. The capacities of NCL@GF-thick and NCL@GF-thin were 244 and 196 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, after 500 cycles at a current density of 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>(Figure S1 c). The NCL@GF with the thick layer is more suitable for sodium storage. Interestingly, we also found that the NCL@GF exhibited an increase in the capacity during long-term cycling, i.e., the capacity delivered 279 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (70 % capacity retention) after 2500 cycles (Figure <a href="#asia201801484-fig-0004">4</a> e), which is superior to the most previously reported carbon-based SIB anode electrodes (140–350 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, Table S1). Even the capacities of the NCL@GF electrode with lower Super P contain (10 % and 0 %) showed almost no decay at a current density of 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>(Figure S7), indicating the NCL@GF with excellent electronic conductivity.</p>
         
         <p>The NCL@GF posses both high discharge capacity and excellent stable performance, which contributes to the unique structure of the NCL@GF. First, N-doping in the carbon framework can supply abundant sites for sodium storage and enhance the electrode kinetics.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0026_R_d8485440e804" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Sci.">26</a>, <a href="#asia201801484-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0028_R_d8485440e804" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Electrochim. Acta">28</a>-<a href="#asia201801484-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0031_R_d8485440e804" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nano Energy">31</a></span> Second, the porous carbon layers on the graphene framework can improve the specific capacity by surface adsorption and faradic reaction, while the hierarchical porous channels can provide facilitate pathways for electrolyte and improve the rate performance.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0012_R_d8485440e806" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Chem.">12</a></span>, <span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0018_R_d8485440e808" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Mater. Chem. A">18</a>, <a href="#asia201801484-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0023_R_d8485440e808" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Sci. Rep.">23</a>, <a href="#asia201801484-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0032_R_d8485440e808" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Nano Energy">32</a>-<a href="#asia201801484-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0035_R_d8485440e808" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Angew. Chem.">35</a></span> Third, the graphene frameworks effectively expose the active surface to the electrolyte but also offer multidimensional electron transport pathway and shorten ion diffusion distance to promote the electrochemical reaction throughout the entire electrode.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0022_R_d8485440e810" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Small">22</a></span> Benefiting from the synergistic effect of N-doping, the unique carbon layer structure and 3D graphene structure, the NCL@GF showed an excellent performance in Na storage applications.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="asia201801484-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="asia201801484-sec-0003-title"> Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>We successfully prepared N-doped porous carbon layers coated on 3D graphene frameworks by polymerization and carbonization methods. The composites exhibited excellent cyclic performance, super rate performance when used as SIB anode materials. This result could be mainly attributed to the synergistic effect of the unique N-doped porous carbon layers and 3D graphene structure with abundant actives sites, porous structure and high conductivity. This work shows a new useful and simple approach for synthesizing N-doping anode materials of SIBs, which displays a novel pathway to the rational design and synthesis of high-performance anode electrodes for rechargeable SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="asia201801484-sec-0004">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="asia201801484-sec-0004-title"> Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p>Preparation of materials: GO was prepared by the modified Hummers method.<span><a href="#asia201801484-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201801484-bib-0036_R_d8485440e829" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">36</a></span> And then the NCL@GF was synthesized by two-steps: First, PMDA (87.2 mg) was dissolved into GO/NMP solution (1 mL, 12 mg mL<sup>−1</sup>), and then added with the equivalent of DAAQ (95.2 mg). Successively, the as-prepared suspension was transferred into a Teflon-lined stainless steel autoclave and heated at 180 °C for 24 h. After cooling down and freeze-drying treatment, the as-prepared materials was carbonized at a high temperature of 600 °C under N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere for 2 h, and the final NCL@GF was obtained. In the control experiments, the different thicknesses of the carbon layer of the NCL@GF were also prepared (NCL@GF-thick, and NCL@GF-thin). The NCL@GF-thick was prepared by adding the same number of monomers into 0.5 mL of GO/NMP solution (24 mg mL<sup>−1</sup>), and the NCL@GF-thin was prepared by adding the monomers into 6 mL of GO/NMP solution (2 mg mL<sup>−1</sup>). The NCF-GF was also prepared by adding the PMDA and EDA. Typically, for the NCF-GF, 87.2 mg of PMDA was dissolved in 2 mL of the 6 mg mL<sup>−1</sup> GO/ NMP solution, and then 24.0 mg of EDA was added to the suspension. The polymerization and carbonization processes were the same as those for the NCL@GF composites. The GF was prepared by the hydrothermal reduction of GO at 180 °C for 24 h and then freeze-drying for 12 h. No N-doped composite CL@GFs were synthesized via the hydrothermal treatment of glucose in the presence of a GO suspension and then carbonization. First, 2 mL of a 5 mg mL<sup>−1</sup> GO suspension and 100 mg of glucose in 2 mL of DI water were mixed and transfered to 50 mL Teflon-lined stainless-steel autoclaves for hydrothermal treatment at 180 °C for 24 h and freeze-drying, Then, the obtained materials were treated at 600 °C in N<sub>2</sub> for 2 h to carbonize the coating layer. For NCF-GF-N<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub>, 43.6 mg of PMDA was dissolved in 4 mL of the 1.5 mg mL<sup>−1</sup> GO/NMP solution. Then, 20 mg of hydrazine hydrate (50 %) was added into the suspension. The polymerization and carbonization processes were the same as those for the NCL@GF composites. The <i>N</i>-GF was prepared via the hydrothermal treatment of solution of PEI (5 mg) and GO (100 mL, 1 mg mL<sup>−1</sup>).</p>
         
         <p><b>Materials characterization</b>: the morphology was tested by a Zeiss Ultra-55 FESEM, an FEI Tecnai G2 20TWIN TEM. The AFM observation measurements were studied with a scanning probe microscope (Multimode 8) operated in tapping mode. FT-IR spectral measurements were recorded on a PerkinElmer Paragon 1000. XRD analysis was carried on a Rigaku D/Max 2500 X-ray diffractometer with Cu<sub>Kα</sub> radiation (<i>λ</i>=1.54 Å) from 10 to 80°. Raman spectra were recorded on a SENTERRA with excitation from the 532 nm line of an Ar-ion laser with a power of about 5 mW. XPS was measured by Thermo 250XI.</p>
         
         <p><b>Electrochemical Measurements</b>: The electrochemical performance was tested in 2016 coin-type cells. The electrode was prepared from the slurry mixture of 70 % active materials, 20 % carbon black (Super P), and 10 % poly(vinyl difluoride) (PVDF) with NMP, and then pasting on the copper foil (99.6 %). We also prepared the control electrodes by mixing the compounds, Super-P, and PVDF with weight ratios of 90:0:10 and 80:10:10. The active material loading in these electrodes was about 1 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The electrolyte was 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in dimethyl carbonate (DMC)/ethylene carbonate (EC) with the volume ratio of 1:1, which was obtained from Ube Industries Ltd. Pure sodium foil was used as the counter electrode. The cells were assembled in an argon-filled glovebox with the concentrations of H<sub>2</sub>O and O<sub>2</sub> below 1 ppm. The cells were tested on LANHE System at various rates within a voltage range from 0.01 to 3 V vs.Na/Na<sup>+</sup> at 25 °C.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
